        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1378134313" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5348073237176677730">I wavered back and forth for a good minute, trying to figure out if I would give this a four star rating or a five star rating. I resolved for five, just because I liked some of the essays more than others. All of them were stunning, but I especially liked Eye to Eye and her account on Moscow, Grenada and raising a black son as a black lesbian.<br/><br/> Audre Lorde has been like a haunt to me in some ways. A name I never felt I could pronounce properly. A name I heard but never read. A book written by a </span>
  <span id="freeText5348073237176677730" style="display:none">I wavered back and forth for a good minute, trying to figure out if I would give this a four star rating or a five star rating. I resolved for five, just because I liked some of the essays more than others. All of them were stunning, but I especially liked Eye to Eye and her account on Moscow, Grenada and raising a black son as a black lesbian.<br /><br /> Audre Lorde has been like a haunt to me in some ways. A name I never felt I could pronounce properly. A name I heard but never read. A book written by a woman with *taboo* identities. A black lesbian woman. It is not a book I would have thought to pick up when I was younger...what would that have to do with me? Why would I read such a thing, so bold in its naturalness and so stunning in its defiance. I have no words to begin to describe the things I felt while reading Lorde. I know for one thing that the regalness that characterizes her name can certainly be backed up by every single word she penned over the years that found its way into Sister Outsider. And what's so amazing about that is Lorde is a poet by trade, and yet her poetic essays are probably some of the best I've ever read. This book is written by a black lesbian, of which she reminds the reader constantly. And this identity is embedded into every word in a way that you cannot forget - which is for the better. Lorde said it best about defining herself and indeed, the way she do so in these essays spoke through so clearly that it may be of relative inconsequence that one may not immediately identify with all her aspects of identity. How precious and life changing a book this is. </span>
  <a data-text-id="5348073237176677730" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1481818907" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1481818907" />
    <a title="El" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/83144-el"><img alt="El" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1479156984p2/83144.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1481818907?book_show_action=true">Dec 27, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="El" class="user" itemprop="url" name="El" href="/user/show/83144-el">El</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83144-el?shelf=cultural-studies-and-other">cultural-studies-and-other</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83144-el?shelf=hear-me-roar-and-gender">hear-me-roar-and-gender</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83144-el?shelf=anarchism-revolutions">anarchism-revolutions</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83144-el?shelf=read-in-a-blink">read-in-a-blink</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1481818907" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17945829802226589569"><blockquote><i>You do not have to be me in order for us to fight alongside each other. I do not have to be you to recognize that our wars are the same. What we must do is commit ourselves to some future that can include each other and to work toward that future with the particular strengths of our individual identities. And in order to do this, we must allow each other our differences at the same time as we recognize our sameness.</i><br/><blockquote>p142, "Learning from the 60s"</blockquote><br/>I read this right on the tail of reading <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/395220.Zami_A_New_Spelling_of_My_Name' title='Zami A New Spelling of My Name by Audre Lorde' rel='nofollow noopener'>Zami: A New</a></blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText17945829802226589569" style="display:none"><blockquote><i>You do not have to be me in order for us to fight alongside each other. I do not have to be you to recognize that our wars are the same. What we must do is commit ourselves to some future that can include each other and to work toward that future with the particular strengths of our individual identities. And in order to do this, we must allow each other our differences at the same time as we recognize our sameness.</i><br /><blockquote>p142, "Learning from the 60s"</blockquote><br />I read this right on the tail of reading <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/395220.Zami_A_New_Spelling_of_My_Name" title="Zami A New Spelling of My Name by Audre Lorde" rel="nofollow noopener">Zami: A New Spelling of My Name</a>, which I loved. I wasn't sure what to expect since this is a collection of speeches and essays written between 1976 and 1984. I wondered if I would find it relevant today.<br /><br />Hoo, boy, did I.<br /><br />There's nothing in Lorde's words that don't resonate with readers (unless their heads and hearts are completely closed and/or missing entirely) even today. The problem is that means all the issues Lorde wrote about still exist. I would love to be able to read this and say "Well, it's so great we don't have to worry about these old-time-thingies!" but that hasn't happened yet, and I don't see it happening anytime soon.<br /><br />Lorde wrote with conviction. She didn't hide her anger, and in fact she offered up the fact that she was angry. But where some people lose their bleeping minds when they get all angry, Lorde never did. She was angry, and she wanted to see things change, but she didn't have unrealistic or unattainable actions for how to accomplish that.<br /><br />I don't want to hear from anyone that they couldn't get something out of this because they're "not black" or "not a lesbian" or "not a mother" or anything else. Everyone can benefit from reading Audre Lorde. Truly inspiring.<br /><blockquote><i>In america, [the mythical norm] is usually defined as white, thin, male, young, heterosexual, christian, and financially secure. It is with this mythical norm that the trappings of power reside within this society. Those of us who stand outside the power often identify one way in which we are different, and we assume that to be the primary cause of all oppression, forgetting other distortions around difference, some of which we ourselves may be practicing.</i><br /><blockquote>p116, "Age, Race, Class, and Sex: Women Redefining Difference"</blockquote><br /></blockquote></blockquote></span>
  <a data-text-id="17945829802226589569" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2076289831" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2076289831" />
    <a title="Kevin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/35434974-kevin"><img alt="Kevin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1571256914p2/35434974.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2076289831?book_show_action=true">May 28, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kevin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kevin" href="/user/show/35434974-kevin">Kevin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781580911863" href="/book/show/730745.Sister_Outsider">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/35434974-kevin?shelf=theory-gender">theory-gender</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2076289831" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6555699252556130754">Lorde was foundational to intersectionality, along with <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/5863103.Angela_Y__Davis' title='Angela Y. Davis' rel='nofollow noopener'>Angela Y. Davis</a> (<a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/635635.Women__Race__and_Class' title='Women, Race, and Class by Angela Y. Davis' rel='nofollow noopener'>Women, Race, and Class</a>).<br/><br/><b>The Good</b>:<br/>--The most striking essays to me are Lorde’s (often directly personal) reflections on dealing with non-<b>intersectional</b> feminism, other black women, and being a black lesbian mother while raising a boy. It seems clear that those who share closer experiences can instantly unlock vaults of memories and understanding from brief passages; for certain gulfs, all I can do is be receptive and appreci</span>
  <span id="freeText6555699252556130754" style="display:none">Lorde was foundational to intersectionality, along with <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/5863103.Angela_Y__Davis" title="Angela Y. Davis" rel="nofollow noopener">Angela Y. Davis</a> (<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/635635.Women__Race__and_Class" title="Women, Race, and Class by Angela Y. Davis" rel="nofollow noopener">Women, Race, and Class</a>).<br /><br /><b>The Good</b>:<br />--The most striking essays to me are Lorde’s (often directly personal) reflections on dealing with non-<b>intersectional</b> feminism, other black women, and being a black lesbian mother while raising a boy. It seems clear that those who share closer experiences can instantly unlock vaults of memories and understanding from brief passages; for certain gulfs, all I can do is be receptive and appreciate the differences.<br />--What made me most excited to explore Lorde’s works was her influence on global/<b>Third-World feminism</b>. There are glimpses of this as the collection starts with Lorde’s visit to Soviet Russia, and ends with her visit to her familial roots of Grenada (post-US invasion). Her critiques of US imperialism/neocolonialism (military and economic terror to enforce ideal conditions for US corporations' global division of labor, as well as racism against black and other coloured self-determination and progress) are brief but on point! <br />…This is what separates Lorde (and Angela Davis, i.e. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/25330108.Freedom_Is_a_Constant_Struggle" title="Freedom Is a Constant Struggle by Angela Y. Davis" rel="nofollow noopener">Freedom Is a Constant Struggle</a>) from those who focus exclusively on domestic US issues and fail to connect with the empire’s global presence. <br /><br /><b>The Missing</b>: <br />--It’s always tricky reviewing early writings on transformative ideas; it doesn’t help that several essays collected are already brief to begin with. <br />--Detailed structural analysis of Third-World feminism/global capitalism/global division of labor (to elaborate on Lorde's "<i>Institutionalized rejection of difference is an absolute necessity in a profit economy which needs outsiders as <b>surplus people</b></i>") will have to be found elsewhere. I need to finish <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/151266.Feminism_without_Borders_Decolonizing_Theory__Practicing_Solidarity" title="Feminism without Borders Decolonizing Theory, Practicing Solidarity by Chandra Talpade Mohanty" rel="nofollow noopener">Feminism without Borders: Decolonizing Theory, Practicing Solidarity</a>; the dense academic writing requires an accessible edition for a wider audience. <br />...Also excited for <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/34448069.Social_Reproduction_Theory_Remapping_Class__Recentring_Oppression" title="Social Reproduction Theory Remapping Class, Recentring Oppression by Tithi Bhattacharya" rel="nofollow noopener">Social Reproduction Theory: Remapping Class, Recentring Oppression</a>, which does have an accessible edition: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/40667455.Feminism_for_the_99__A_Manifesto" title="Feminism for the 99% A Manifesto by Cinzia Arruzza" rel="nofollow noopener">Feminism for the 99%: A Manifesto</a>.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6555699252556130754" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1894239371" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1894239371" />
    <a title="Barbara (The Bibliophage)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3932962-barbara-the-bibliophage"><img alt="Barbara (The Bibliophage)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1277695778p2/3932962.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1894239371?book_show_action=true">Apr 08, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Barbara (The Bibliophage)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Barbara (The Bibliophage)" href="/user/show/3932962-barbara-the-bibliophage">Barbara (The Bibliophage)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3932962-barbara-the-bibliophage?shelf=2017">2017</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3932962-barbara-the-bibliophage?shelf=social-justice">social-justice</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3932962-barbara-the-bibliophage?shelf=own-digital">own-digital</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3932962-barbara-the-bibliophage?shelf=audio">audio</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3932962-barbara-the-bibliophage?shelf=feminism-and-women-s-studies">feminism-and-women-s-studies</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3932962-barbara-the-bibliophage?shelf=diverse-authors">diverse-authors</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3932962-barbara-the-bibliophage?shelf=own">own</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1894239371" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12993923152507466008">Sister Outsider was originally compiled in 1984 from poet Audre Lorde's speeches and essays. I listened to the audiobook produced in 2016, and narrated by Robin Eller. Let's get my critique of the audio out of the way - it was deplorable. Lorde's passionate and insightful words are ruined by narration that's closer to automaton than human. Buy the print version, as I will be doing soon. <br/><br/>That frustration aside, this book is a powerful work. Lorde's experiences as a black, feminist, lesbian mother</span>
  <span id="freeText12993923152507466008" style="display:none">Sister Outsider was originally compiled in 1984 from poet Audre Lorde's speeches and essays. I listened to the audiobook produced in 2016, and narrated by Robin Eller. Let's get my critique of the audio out of the way - it was deplorable. Lorde's passionate and insightful words are ruined by narration that's closer to automaton than human. Buy the print version, as I will be doing soon. <br /><br />That frustration aside, this book is a powerful work. Lorde's experiences as a black, feminist, lesbian mother of two afford her a valuable perspective. For me, one of the most affecting essays was when she talked about black women failing to connect with and support other black women. While much of this was specific to the black woman's experience, I thought many points applied to all women. <br /><br /><b>"If I participate, knowingly or otherwise, in my sister's oppression and she calls me on it, to answer her anger with my own only blankets the substance of our exchange with reaction. It wastes energy. And yes, it is very difficult to stand still and to listen to another woman's voice delineate an agony I do not share, or one to which I myself have contributed.”</b><br /><br />Lorde describes white privilege and oppression so strongly. Her essays were a profound extension of the recent reading I've done (Malcolm X, Ralph Ellison, Congressman John Lewis, and Janet Mock).<br /><br /><b>“The way you get people to testify against themselves is not to have police tactics and oppressive techniques. What you do is build it in so people learn to distrust everything in themselves that has not been sanctioned, to reject what is most creative in themselves to begin with, so you don’t even need to stamp it out.”</b><br /><br />I loved her essay, Manchild, about raising a black son from an interracial lesbian household in a racist world. She also shares some precious and gut-wrenching moments of watching her daughter adjust to life on her own.<br /><br />Lastly, Lorde's essay about the 1983 U.S. invasion of Grenada makes points about the politics of aggression which continue to be valid today. I happened to listen to it in a two-day period where the U.S. twice made aggressive moves towards non-white countries. I wonder what Lorde would say about the military events of the last 20 years?<br /><br /><b>“What gets me about the United States is that it pretends to be honest and therefore has so little room to move toward hope.”</b><br /><br />Meaningful as this all is, the quote that hit me the most was this one:<br /><br /><b>“Tell them about how you’re never really a whole person if you remain silent, because there’s always that one little piece inside you that wants to be spoken out, and if you keep ignoring it, it gets madder and madder and hotter and hotter, and if you don’t speak it out one day it will just up and punch you in the mouth from the inside.”</b><br /><br />Read these essays for a unique voice, which is well worth your time.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12993923152507466008" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2779682441" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2779682441" />
    <a title="Dominic" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2551271-dominic"><img alt="Dominic" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1578105289p2/2551271.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2779682441?book_show_action=true">Apr 10, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dominic" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dominic" href="/user/show/2551271-dominic">Dominic</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781580911863" href="/book/show/730745.Sister_Outsider">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2551271-dominic?shelf=feminism">feminism</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2551271-dominic?shelf=nonfic">nonfic</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2779682441" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6750731961255897532">I'm shook. While I've read several of these essays in my college days (and beyond), finally reading this iconic collection in its entirety is to fully understand the scope of Audre Lorde's goodness, her vulnerability and her warrior-queer-rebel genius. I have not read any full poetry collections from Lorde, but I really damn well should, because Lorde may be one of the most visionary revolutionaries I've ever read.<br/><br/>What impresses me most about Lorde is how she can incite action while making room </span>
  <span id="freeText6750731961255897532" style="display:none">I'm shook. While I've read several of these essays in my college days (and beyond), finally reading this iconic collection in its entirety is to fully understand the scope of Audre Lorde's goodness, her vulnerability and her warrior-queer-rebel genius. I have not read any full poetry collections from Lorde, but I really damn well should, because Lorde may be one of the most visionary revolutionaries I've ever read.<br /><br />What impresses me most about Lorde is how she can incite action while making room in the revolution for everyone. A repeated motif throughout these essays and speeches is that our differences make us stronger. Rather than shoot out a cliche aphorism, though, Lorde probes and refuses to give into complacency. As she makes clear over and over, people are dying; there is no time but now. She doesn't let anyone off easy; her words are honest and blunt and raw.<br /><br />Although most of these writings are from the late 70s and early 80s, they are incredibly timeless. This isn't a text to read for a historical perspective on feminism "back-in-the-day." Lorde's wisdom feels as relevant today as ever before—we need to be discussing these ideas and how they can help heal wounds and stir up resistance. This is rage couched with a belief that people can come together and connect. It's inspiring that someone who faced racism, sexism and homophobia so constantly could armor herself with such hope and resilience and courage. That's the power of poetry, y'all. (There are plenty of fightin' words here as well.)<br /><br />Although she is quoted in the introduction, saying, "I'm a poet [;] I don't write theory," it turns out that Lorde is the very BEST kind of theorist because she doesn't just describe how things are but how we can envision it to be. And Lorde's vision is so generous! My favourite quote can be found on page 43, from "The Transformation of Silence into Language and Action":<br /><br /><blockquote>Where the words of women are crying to be heard, we must each of us recognize our responsibility to seek those words out, to read them and share them and examine them in their pertinence to our lives. That we not hide behind the mockeries of separations that have been imposed upon us and which so often we accept as our own."</blockquote><br /><br />I adore that insight. It challenges me to continue to learn about and read about difference. It reinforces the fact that I have learned so many things from people whose perspective is far different from mind. I love that she says this is our "responsibility" to listen. Anyone who says our differences mean that we can't learn from another or interact with another or fight alongside one another is one of those tools of the Master's house. And we don't need to be listening to that.<br /><br />Thank you, Audre Lorde, for your words, which are a true blessing to me.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6750731961255897532" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2032764711" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2032764711" />
    <a title="Viv JM" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4243437-viv-jm"><img alt="Viv JM" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1487793618p2/4243437.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2032764711?book_show_action=true">Jun 25, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Viv JM" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Viv JM" href="/user/show/4243437-viv-jm">Viv JM</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audio CD 9781515955436" href="/book/show/29458886-sister-outsider">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4243437-viv-jm?shelf=z-2017-author-female">z-2017-author-female</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4243437-viv-jm?shelf=audio">audio</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4243437-viv-jm?shelf=essay">essay</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4243437-viv-jm?shelf=feminism">feminism</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4243437-viv-jm?shelf=rt-challenge-jun-17">rt-challenge-jun-17</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4243437-viv-jm?shelf=author-female">author-female</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4243437-viv-jm?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4243437-viv-jm?shelf=read-in-2017">read-in-2017</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2032764711" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14227653123834418785">Audre Lorde (1934-1992) was a black lesbian feminist poet and “Sister Outsider” is a collection of her essays and speeches dating from 1976 to 1984. These are largely on themes of sexism, racism and homophobia and Lorde is not afraid to express her anger. Reading these essays 40 years later feels a little bit depressing as although a lot has changed there is so much more that hasn’t. <br/><br/>I did find this at times an uncomfortable read, and I think that is wholly a good thing. It feels like a really i</span>
  <span id="freeText14227653123834418785" style="display:none">Audre Lorde (1934-1992) was a black lesbian feminist poet and “Sister Outsider” is a collection of her essays and speeches dating from 1976 to 1984. These are largely on themes of sexism, racism and homophobia and Lorde is not afraid to express her anger. Reading these essays 40 years later feels a little bit depressing as although a lot has changed there is so much more that hasn’t. <br /><br />I did find this at times an uncomfortable read, and I think that is wholly a good thing. It feels like a really important book for white feminists to read to help understand the need for more intersectionality and to acknowledge that our experiences as women are not universal. <br /><br />The last essay in this book is about the American invasion of Grenada, which I feel ashamed to say I knew nothing about previously. <br /><br />I listened to the audiobook version of this, narrated by Robin Eller. I can’t say that I would recommend the format, partly because I found Eller’s narration somewhat flat but also because it’s the sort of book that needs highlighting, re-reading and taking notes. I will definitely be on the lookout for a print copy.<br /></span>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer9161537890742885616">1. A collection of essays and speeches by Audre Lorde. A lot of people have read this since she is definitely one of the most important and influential women in history. Which is why my words on it aren't really that important because it's no different than anyone else's.<br/><br/>2. I don't read a lot of non-fiction but this was written in a way where I could easily digest it without clawing my eyes out. It is written in her lovely voice by her lovely fingers.<br/><br/>3.   <span id="freeText9161537890742885616" style="display:none">1. A collection of essays and speeches by Audre Lorde. A lot of people have read this since she is definitely one of the most important and influential women in history. Which is why my words on it aren't really that important because it's no different than anyone else's.<br /><br />2. I don't read a lot of non-fiction but this was written in a way where I could easily digest it without clawing my eyes out. It is written in her lovely voice by her lovely fingers.<br /><br />3. She is delicate and strong, knowing that she is shoved outside of everything by every sister.<br /><br />4. She is a women and a feminist.<br /><br />5. A Black women and a Lesbian.<br /><br />6. A poet and a word weaver.<br /><br />7. Writing is a form of healing for those who know they are consistently clawed out of existence or muted.<br /><br />8. Sisters should always join hands but yet we never do it.<br /><br />9. You know that overused quote about pens being a weapon? This book is one of the most powerful swords, it will rip every form of organism and matter with one slice.<br /><br />10. And that all I have to say. By the way, I wonder if she has a book dedicated to her travels, I really liked that first essay. Of course, I loved all of the other ones, but that one stuck with me for some reason, the way she describes people and landscapes, I guess that's just how her poetic mind influences her prose.<br /><br />Rating: 5/5<br /><br />Originally posted here: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://wordsnotesandfiction.blogspot.com/2015/07/listicle-reviews-for-two-books-my.html">http://wordsnotesandfiction.blogspot....</a></span>

<div id="review_2296104985" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2296104985" />
    <a title="Never Without a Book" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/45952189-never-without-a-book"><img alt="Never Without a Book" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1556991437p2/45952189.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2296104985?book_show_action=true">Feb 23, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Never Without a Book" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Never Without a Book" href="/user/show/45952189-never-without-a-book">Never Without a Book</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2296104985" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17361025838541548865">I was introduce to Audre years ago, but I never read any of her work. I'm not sure why I waited so long, but Sister Outsider was absolutely fantastic. What blows my mind about this book is that it was written in the 80's and sad to say not much has change when it comes to sexism, racism, ageism and homophobia today. Not only was Lorde a poet she was an activist of<br/>hate crimes. One of the essay Lorde wrote that I found very interesting was the story of 20 year old Patricia Cowan. Patricia was an a</span>
  <span id="freeText17361025838541548865" style="display:none">I was introduce to Audre years ago, but I never read any of her work. I'm not sure why I waited so long, but Sister Outsider was absolutely fantastic. What blows my mind about this book is that it was written in the 80's and sad to say not much has change when it comes to sexism, racism, ageism and homophobia today. Not only was Lorde a poet she was an activist of<br />hate crimes. One of the essay Lorde wrote that I found very interesting was the story of 20 year old Patricia Cowan. Patricia was an aspiring actress who auditioned for a lead role in a play called “Hammer”. She was bludgeoned to death by the playwright an African-American man<br />with sledge hammer. This story blew my mind! I highly recommend this book, excellent book from a phenomenal woman.</span>

          </span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('Sdg1rvPsNjvBMw9KyG94u4xDmzNDFnvZpikVYvbVDi2jb0WjfpfHTY+DaPfblDpF9Ea7edn3+9nGyHuGFoKiWw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('btVvrXrnKtE4Y3PUH8OsVZDsxSJjKilEg+eJNFQqUtGEYh+g95zbp3bTFGkMOO6r6OnlaPnLqUTjBufQtH3+pw==')})
  }

Davis has put the case for the latest abolition movement in American life: the abolition of the prison. As she quite correctly notes, American life is replete with abolition movements, and when they were engaged in these struggles, their chances of success seemed almost unthinkable. For generations of Americans, the abolition of slavery was sheerest illusion. Similarly,the entrenched system of racial segregation seemed to last forever, and generations lived in the midst of the practice, with few predicting its passage from custom. The brutal, exploitative (dare one say lucrative?) convict-lease system that succeeded formal slavery reaped millions to southern jurisdictions (and untold miseries for tens of thousands of men, and women). Few predicted its passing from the American penal landscape. Davis expertly argues how social movements transformed these social, political and cultural institutions, and made such practices untenable.In Are Prisons Obsolete?, Professor Davis seeks to illustrate that the time for the prison is approaching an end. Long a target of J. Edgar Hoover\'s campaign to defame, infiltrate, and criminalize Black nationalist organizations and their leaders, Shakur was incarcerated for four years prior to her conviction on flimsy evidence in 1977 as an accomplice to murder.\n\nThis intensely personal and political autobiography belies the fearsome image of JoAnne Chesimard long projected by the media and the state. With wit and candor, Assata Shakur recounts the experiences that led her to a life of activism and portrays the strengths, weaknesses, and eventual demise of Black and White revolutionary groups at the hand of government officials. The result is a signal contribution to the literature about growing up Black in America that has already taken its place alongside The Autobiography of Malcolm X and the works of Maya Angelou.\n\nTwo years after her conviction, Assata Shakur escaped from prison. Some are visible, some are hidden—but all are underrepresented in media and popular culture. Now, just in time for the thirtieth anniversary of the Americans with Disabilities Act, activist Alice Wong brings together an urgent, galvanizing collection of personal essays by contemporary disabled writers. There is Harriet McBryde Johnson’s “Unspeakable Conversations,” which describes her famous debate with Princeton philosopher Peter Singer over her own personhood. There is columnist s. e. smith’s celebratory review of a work of theater by disabled performers. There are original pieces by up-and-coming authors like Keah Brown and Haben Girma. There are blog posts, manifestos, eulogies, and testimonies to Congress. Taken together, this anthology gives a glimpse of the vast richness and complexity of the disabled experience, highlighting the passions, talents, and everyday lives of this community. It invites readers to question their own assumptions and understandings. It celebrates and documents disability culture in the now.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/18486.Audre_Lorde">About Audre Lorde</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/18486.Audre_Lorde"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1591713286p3/18486.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/18486.Audre_Lorde">Audre Lorde
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
2,519
followers
</div>

</div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer7114447578899113183">Audre Lorde is a revolutionary Black feminist. Lorde's poetry was published very regularly during the 1960s — in Langston Hughes' 1962 New Negro Poets, USA; in several foreign anthologies; and in black literary magazines. During this time, she was politically active in civil rights, anti-war, and feminist movements. Her first volume of poetry, The First Cities (1968), was published by the Poet's P</span>
  <span id="freeText7114447578899113183" style="display:none">Audre Lorde is a revolutionary Black feminist. Lorde's poetry was published very regularly during the 1960s — in Langston Hughes' 1962 New Negro Poets, USA; in several foreign anthologies; and in black literary magazines. During this time, she was politically active in civil rights, anti-war, and feminist movements. Her first volume of poetry, The First Cities (1968), was published by the Poet's Press and edited by Diane di Prima, a former classmate and friend from Hunter College High School. Dudley Randall, a poet and critic, asserted in his review of the book that Lorde "does not wave a black flag, but her blackness is there, implicit, in the bone."<br /><br />Her second volume, Cables to Rage (1970), which was mainly written during her tenure at Tougaloo College in Mississippi, addressed themes of love, betrayal, childbirth and the complexities of raising children. It is particularly noteworthy for the poem "Martha", in which Lorde poetically confirms her homosexuality: "[W]e shall love each other here if ever at all." Later books continued her political aims in lesbian and gay rights, and feminism. In 1980, together with Barbara Smith and Cherríe Moraga, she co-founded Kitchen Table: Women of Color Press, the first U.S. publisher for women of colour. Lorde was State Poet of New York from 1991 to 1992.<br /><br /><a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Audre_Lorde" rel="nofollow noopener">Read More</a><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="7114447578899113183" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
